Lesson applies any time you hand-compose ANSI-framed output instead of letting Rich's layout engine handle spacing.
- **`rich.prompt.Prompt.ask(..., default="")` renders an empty `()` in the label.** (Session 122.) The prompt text showed as `tagline (one line, under ~60 chars) ():` — Rich always appends `(default_value)` to the label when a default is set, and an empty-string default renders as an empty pair of parens. Fix: pass `show_default=False`. Non-obvious because the default value *itself* is correct and everything works — it's just cosmetic noise in the label.
- **`claude -p` in a non-interactive subprocess defaults to permission mode `ask` — which denies every Write/Edit/Bash.** (Session 125.) `delegate_to_claude_code` MCP spawns `claude -p <task> --worktree` as a stdio subprocess. In TTY-less contexts `ask` mode has no human to confirm with and auto-denies, so every file edit inside the worktree silently fails — the subprocess exits "success" but the tool list is effectively read-only. Surfaced only once we test-drove the harness: Engineer kept reporting "I wasn't able to write that file" for trivially-writable tasks. Fix: pass `--permission-mode bypassPermissions` explicitly (safe because Operator already confirm-gates each delegate call and `--worktree` throwaway-isolates the fs). Made the mode configurable via `DELEGATE_PERMISSION_MODE` env var in `agents/engineer/config.yaml → mcp_servers.delegate.env` so users who want a tighter sandbox can pick `acceptEdits` (file ops yes, Bash no). Lesson: whenever an LLM CLI is spawned as a subprocess and behaves like it has fewer tools than expected, check the permission mode first — it's almost always the culprit and the symptom (silent no-op) looks like anything but a permission issue.
- **LLM routes local-path questions to GitHub MCP when it has both `github__*` and `delegate` tools available.** (Session 125.) Engineer's first harness session: asked "how many python files are in pipeline/?", model called `github__search_code(query='path:pipeline/ extension:py', repo='<guessed>/<guessed>')` — hit 404s, didn't recover. It has no way to know "this path is local not remote" from tool schemas alone; github's description sounds plausible for any repo-ish question. Fix: add an explicit tool-routing paragraph to the bot's `llm.system_prompt`: `github__*` is for *remote* repos the user names by owner/repo; any local path = delegate to Claude Code; "when in doubt, prefer delegate." Generalizable: whenever two tools have overlapping surface area (both can "read code"), you need a routing rule in the prompt — tool descriptions alone aren't enough signal.
- **`ChatRunner` treats `connector._connected = False` as an unexpected disconnect.** (Session 125.) First `TerminalConnector` draft implemented `/quit` by flipping `_connected` to `False` — clean stop, right? But `ChatRunner._loop` calls `connector.is_connected()` every tick and on `False` prints "Meeting connection lost" plus the reconnection-attempt warnings from the Meet disconnect path. Visible as noise on the user's final `/quit`. Fix: route stdin `/quit`/EOF through `os.kill(os.getpid(), signal.SIGINT)` to trigger the existing SIGINT handler cleanly — same exit path as Ctrl-C. Generalizable: any new connector should distinguish "user asked to leave" (signal) from "connection died" (flag). Only the second one is a `_connected = False`.
- **`claude -p --resume <id>` looks up sessions by cwd, and must NOT be combined with `--worktree`.** (Session 126.) Setting up per-repo continuity in the delegate MCP meant pairing `--worktree` on first call with `--resume` on follow-ups. Two non-obvious traps: (a) running `claude -p ... --resume <id>` from the *parent repo* cwd errors with `No conversation found with session ID: <id>` — the session registry is indexed by project dir, and `--worktree` on first call changed the project dir to the worktree, so resume only works when cwd = the worktree path; (b) passing both `--resume` and `--worktree` on the same invocation does NOT reuse the original sandbox — `--worktree` spawns a brand-new one. Compounding: `claude --resume` with a bad session id exits 0 and writes the error to *stdout* as plain text, not JSON — so our JSON parser has to treat non-JSON stdout as an error path (not just stderr or non-zero exit). Fix: on resume, `cwd=worktree_path`, command has `--resume <id>` only, no `--worktree`. And on any non-JSON stdout containing "No conversation found", fall back to spawning a fresh `--worktree` call (handles the case where the user manually deleted the worktree dir between calls). Surfaced during verification in `/tmp/delegate_verify/` before committing the rewrite — first resume attempt from the parent repo failed silently from the MCP's POV (empty result), only the raw `claude` CLI run showed the "No conversation found" line.
- **`git diff` ignores untracked files by default — `land_worktree_changes` needs `git add -A` first.** (Session 126.) Claude Code's subprocess consistently leaves new files it creates **untracked** in the worktree (it only commits on explicit request). `land_worktree_changes` was computing `git -C <worktree> diff <base_sha>` to produce a patch for `git apply`, and the diff was empty even when the sandbox clearly had new files — because `git diff` (without `--cached` or `HEAD`) compares the working-tree version of *tracked* files against the index, and untracked files aren't in either. First landing attempt returned "No changes to land" for a sandbox containing an untracked `marker.txt` with content. Fix: `git -C <worktree> add -A` before the diff, then `git diff --cached <base_sha>` — staging pulls untracked files into the index so they appear in the diff. Side-effect-free because worktrees are throwaway sandboxes; staging them doesn't affect the parent repo's index or anywhere the user would notice.
- **Terminal harness pins `get_participant_count()` to 2 to force 1-on-1 mode.** (Session 125.) `ChatRunner` requires the `@operator` trigger phrase in rooms with >`ONE_ON_ONE_THRESHOLD` (=2) participants. A terminal session has one human and one bot — conceptually 2 participants. Returning 1 would trip the alone-exit grace timer. Returning anything ≥3 would force the user to type `@operator` before every message, breaking the test-drive UX. Pinning to exactly 2 is the sweet spot. Worth capturing because it's a non-obvious coupling between a "test harness" and the meeting-mode logic — future connectors (Slack? Discord? CLI chat?) all face the same decision.
- **MagicMock falls through `or` as truthy — mock the method or the `or` default never fires.** (Session 129.) Added `MCPClient.tool_timeout_for(tool_name)` and wired `chat_runner` to read it as `self._mcp.tool_timeout_for(tc["name"]) or config.TOOL_TIMEOUT_SECONDS`. Unit tests started failing with `TypeError: '<=' not supported between instances of 'MagicMock' and 'int'`. Cause: test mocks built via `MagicMock()` auto-create the new method as another MagicMock, which is **truthy**, so `or` short-circuits on it and returns the mock itself — which then fails integer comparison downstream in the heartbeat/timeout math. Fix: explicit `mcp.tool_timeout_for.return_value = None` in every fixture that builds a MagicMock'd MCP. Generalizable: any time you add a new method to an interface that's MagicMock'd in tests, every existing fixture needs an explicit stub for that method — MagicMock's "just works" default behavior is exactly what trips `or`/`and`/truthy-check fallbacks.
- **`idle_timeout_seconds` wasn't what it sounded like.** (Session 129.) Config had an `idle_timeout_seconds: 600` under `connector:` on every bot. Name implied "how long Operator waits in an idle browser before giving up." grep showed the constant was actually the max wait for the user to admit the bot from the Meet lobby (`connectors/macos_adapter.py:354` — a polling loop during the knock/admit phase). Two wrongs compound: the name doesn't describe the behavior, and power users adjust it to cover a different scenario than the one it controls. Fix: renamed to `LOBBY_WAIT_SECONDS` in `config.py`, updated both connector callsites. Lesson: any config key still wearing a name from an earlier architecture is tech debt — grep its reads when auditing, don't trust the name.
- **`git commit` without `-a` silently drops unstaged edits after `git mv`.** (Session 143.) The templates → agents collapse did four things: `git mv src/_1_800_operator/templates/agents src/_1_800_operator/agents` (17 renames auto-staged by git), Edit the two `_TEMPLATES_DIR` → `_BUNDLED_AGENTS_DIR` Python references, Edit `pyproject.toml` comment, Edit `docs/roadmap.md` row. Ran `git commit -m ...` — commit landed as "17 files changed, 0 insertions, 0 deletions" (all renames), and the 4 Edit-tool changes were left dangling in the working tree, unstaged. What made it tricky: every verification step passed. Tests ran against the working tree (where edits were applied), wheel built correctly (same), install round-trip worked (same). Only caught because a post-commit `git status --short` still showed 4 dirty files. The broken commit `3d8582a` in isolation would import-error on first run — the directory was renamed but `_TEMPLATES_DIR = .../templates/agents` still pointed at the old path. Fix: follow-up commit `3e4f23e` bundled the dangling edits with a message explaining the atomic intent belonged with `3d8582a`. Not amended per user's append-only history preference. Lesson: when a commit mixes `git mv` (auto-staged) with file edits from other tools, always run `git status --short` before `git commit` to see what's staged vs modified — or use `git commit -a` to capture tracked-file modifications. "Tests pass" is not sufficient signal because tests run against the working tree, not the staged index.
- **`pipdeptree --reverse` silently omits parents that use extras.** (Session 144.) Dep audit: `pipdeptree --reverse --packages cryptography` in a clean install of the 7 pinned direct deps returned "no reverse dependents" even though `cryptography==46.0.7` was clearly installed. Initially looked like pip had installed an orphan. Root cause: `mcp==1.27.0` declares `pyjwt[crypto]>=2.10.1`, and the `[crypto]` extra pulls `cryptography>=3.4.0`. Extras-based parent links live in the package's `requires_dist` metadata with an `extra == "crypto"` marker, not in the flat `Requires:` field `pipdeptree` reads. The tool had no way to see the edge, so it presented cryptography as rootless. What made it tricky: the symptom ("nothing requires this, yet it's installed") is the same symptom a truly phantom dep would produce — so it's easy to mistake a real transitive for a phantom and drop it. Fix/method: use `pip install --dry-run --report <file>.json` instead — the JSON report includes every resolved package's full `requires_dist` list, including `extra == "..."` markers, so the real parent chain becomes visible. Generalizable rule: whenever `pipdeptree --reverse` says "no parent" for something pip clearly chose to install, don't believe it — re-verify via the install report before declaring a dep phantom. Confirmed phantom in this session: `requests` (absent from the resolved tree, floor dropped). Confirmed real via the report: `cryptography`.
- **`~/.operator/agents/` shadows the bundled template after first run — bundle edits go stale until you nuke the user dir.** (Session 146.) Did a `delegate → claude-code` yaml-key rename across all three bundled configs in `src/_1_800_operator/agents/`, confirmed by file inspection + test suite. But `OPERATOR_BOT=engineer python -c "from operator import config; print(list(config.MCP_SERVERS.keys()))"` still returned `['delegate', 'github']` after every re-import. Not a caching issue — `config.py` reads `~/.operator/agents/<name>/config.yaml`, and the user-level copy had been written on an earlier `operator` run via the first-run auto-copy hook (session-142 behavior, seed-once-then-own). After that first copy, the user dir is the source of truth; the bundled template is just a seed for fresh installs. What made it tricky: the bundle was clearly correct, tests (which read the src-layout path directly) passed, but runtime looked completely unaffected by the rename. User asked to `rm -rf ~/.operator/agents/`, next `operator` invocation re-ran the auto-copy from the updated bundle, MCPs loaded as `['claude-code', 'github']` on the first try. Generalizable: any time a bundle edit "doesn't take" at runtime, check `~/.operator/agents/` — the user-level copies are almost certainly shadowing it. Dev-loop rule for template edits: `rm -rf ~/.operator/agents/` after the edit, OR point `_AGENTS_DIR` at a temp directory in tests that need to exercise the auto-copy path. Also a real UX concern for users: they'll hit the same thing if they pull a new Operator version that changes a bundled config, because the update won't reach their user dir without an explicit refresh.
- **String-literal CLI-surface audits miss positional-argv test shapes.** (Session 150.) The 15.8.0 audit of the bare `operator <name>` form grepped for literal strings like `operator pm` / `operator {name}` / `operator <name>` across the tree and declared `tests/test_entry_cli.py` a zero-hit category because no assertion message contains that phrase. Wrong: `test_main_known_bot_dispatches_to_run_bot` and `test_main_unknown_bot_returns_2` both exercised the bare form as `patched_argv(["pm", "<url>"])` and `patched_argv(["ghost"])` — positional argv lists no string-literal grep catches. What made it tricky: the files *were* in the audit scope and skimmed, but the eye scans for string matches of the rename pattern, not for argv-list shapes whose semantics depend on the dispatcher. Fix was mechanical (rename + add 3 tests for the new dispatcher branches). Lesson: when auditing a CLI surface for rename, every test file that exercises `main()` or the dispatcher needs a semantic read (what shape of argv does this pass? what dispatch branch does it cover?) not just a string-match pass. Better audit prompt: "find every place that invokes the CLI — strings OR argv-structured."
- **Claude Code's real MCP list lives in `claude mcp list`, not `~/.claude.json#mcpServers`.** (Session 151.) Initial design for the `claude` bundled agent's auto-import assumed user MCPs would be in `~/.claude.json#mcpServers` — the canonical Claude Code settings file. Wrote the JSON-parse path first, unit-tested it, then ran it against the author's real home and got **zero MCPs** despite the user obviously having Gmail + Drive + Calendar + Linear connected. Dug into `~/.claude.json` and found the `mcpServers` key literally empty; the 4 active MCPs lived only in `claude mcp list` output because they're claude.ai *account-level* connectors (configured in the claude.ai UI, not in any local JSON). Projects section had `enabledMcpjsonServers` referencing project-scoped MCPs but nothing at the user level. What made it tricky: the JSON path worked correctly and tested cleanly — the bug was "tested against the wrong source of truth." Fix: added a second discovery path that shells out to `claude mcp list` and regex-parses the text output (10s timeout, non-match skip, degrades to empty on format drift). Hosted URLs auto-wrap via `mcp-remote@0.1.38`. Lesson: when integrating with another tool's config, don't assume the config file is the source of truth — many modern CLIs have hybrid local-file + account-backend state, and the *authoritative* list is whatever the tool itself reports when you ask it. Always verify against a real user's setup before freezing the API shape. Fragility risk: if Claude Code ever changes the `claude mcp list` output format, imports silently return zero — Phase 17 upstream-drift watch should monitor `_CLAUDE_MCP_LIST_RE` in `pipeline/claude_code_import.py`.
- **OAuth-over-mcp-remote surfaces no sniffable error on missing/cancelled auth.** (Session 148.) Empirical probe in 15.7.1a: boot `npx -y mcp-remote https://mcp.linear.app/sse` with an empty cache dir, call a tool. Browser pops open with OAuth URL; stdio `session.initialize()` stalls indefinitely until our explicit 45s timeout fires with a `TimeoutError`. User clicking **Cancel** on the Linear popup produces the same stall — no "auth denied" error travels back through the MCP channel. What made it important: the whole 15.7.1d sniff list was originally conceived to handle Linear-style auth failures, but Linear has no string to sniff. Reshaped scope: runtime sniff covers API-key MCPs only; OAuth MCPs are detected proactively via 15.7.3 cache-path check. Generalizable: whenever a pre-meeting auth check is being designed, assume any `mcp-remote`-backed server can only be detected by looking at its token cache on disk, not by interpreting runtime errors.
- **Renaming beats retyping when changing a dict's value shape.** (Session 148.) 15.7.1 had to upgrade `MCPClient.failed_servers: dict[str, str]` to `dict[str, dict]`. Python won't flag a type change — code like `for name, reason in failed_servers.items(): log.error(reason)` keeps running but prints `{'kind': ..., 'vars': [...]}` as a stringified dict into the log. Chose to rename to `startup_failures` (and `disabled_servers` → `runtime_failures`) instead. Every stale consumer now raises `AttributeError` on first access — `__main__.py`, `chat_runner.py`, and two test files all surfaced immediately at test time and got updated in one pass. No "found a stringified dict in logs three weeks later" surprise. Generalizable: dynamic-typed languages hide shape changes; when you can't rely on the type checker, use the name as a tripwire.
- **mcp-remote's token cache key = `md5(auth_url)` where `auth_url` is the URL mcp-remote hashes internally — which may differ from the positional arg.** (Session 148.) Confirmed by reading `dist/chunk-*.js` at `~/.npm/_npx/.../mcp-remote/`: `getServerUrlHash(serverUrl, authorizeResource, headers)` returns `md5([serverUrl, authorizeResource?, headers?].join("|"))`. For the no-headers/no-authorize_resource happy path (every server we ship), it's just `md5(serverUrl)`. Subtle catch verified against local disk: the existing tokens file is `~/.mcp-auth/mcp-remote-0.1.37/fcc436b0d1e0a1ed9a2b15bbd638eb13_tokens.json`, which is `md5("https://mcp.linear.app/mcp")` — **not** `md5` of the `/sse` URL the config passes as the positional arg. The probe lockfile (session-scoped) matches the `/sse` hash, but the tokens cache uses `/mcp`. Best guess: http-first transport strategy normalizes to `/mcp` internally before caching. For the check to work reliably, the bundled config needs an explicit `auth_url` field that the bundle author declares, not derived from args. Two other gotchas while implementing: (a) version dir (`mcp-remote-0.1.37`) bumps on package upgrade so cache is per-version — locally-upgraded mcp-remote creates a fresh dir and effectively forces re-auth until the user reruns `operator auth linear`; (b) existence ≠ validity — a revoked or refresh-failed token still has a file on disk, so the check is "will mcp-remote hang at boot waiting for browser OAuth" not "will the next tool call succeed." Runtime 401 sniff (15.7.1d) handles the second half orthogonally for API-key MCPs; for OAuth MCPs it's still a runtime surprise if the refresh token has expired.
- **MCP spec has no semantic error taxonomy — every host does substring sniffing.** (Session 148.) The JSON-RPC substrate has standardized protocol-level errors (parse error, method not found, invalid params), but application-level failure shapes are entirely server-author-defined. `isError: true` tool results ship as free-form text (GitHub "Bad credentials", Figma "Forbidden", Slack "invalid_auth", Sentry "Invalid token", Google gRPC "UNAUTHENTICATED", etc.) with no machine-readable `error_code` field. Every MCP host in the ecosystem (Claude Desktop, Cursor, Continue) runs substring sniffing for auth-like patterns — it's the state of the art, not a workaround. Implication for future bundle additions: whenever adding a new MCP, re-run `tests/probe_auth_errors.py` against it with a bogus credential and append captured strings to `docs/mcp-auth-errors.md` + `_AUTH_ERROR_PATTERNS`. Accept that false negatives ship downgraded UX (LLM relays verbatim instead of banner showing "re-auth"), not silent failure.
- **Bundled MCP `command`/`args` that are relative paths break silently the moment CWD assumptions shift.** (Session 154.) The `claude` agent's `claude-code` MCP had `command: python, args: ["agents/engineer/claude_code.py"]` — worked for a year because the launcher `cd`'s to REPO_DIR before dispatch, so the relative path resolved against the repo root. Post-src-layout packaging the file moved to `src/_1_800_operator/agents/engineer/claude_code.py`; the relative path no longer matched; MCP subprocess died with `[Errno 2] No such file or directory` at spawn. Same class of bug had been sitting in all four bundled agents as `command: "./github-mcp-server"` for a separate Go binary — dormant today because it was only `enabled: true` on engineer and the launcher's `cd` was masking it. Both bugs would detonate under `uv tool install` (Phase 14.5) because the installed CLI's CWD is the user's invocation dir, not any reliable repo root. What made it tricky: no existing test exercised bundled configs end-to-end. `test_mcp_client.py` spawned a *synthetic* test server at an absolute tmp path; `test_config_loader.py` validated YAML→dict parsing with *synthetic* YAML; neither one loaded `src/_1_800_operator/agents/*/config.yaml`. Fixes applied: `args: ["-m", "_1_800_operator.agents.engineer.claude_code"]` for the python script (namespace-package import works without `__init__.py` under PEP 420; CWD-independent in dev and installed); `command: "github-mcp-server"` (bare name, PATH lookup) for the Go binary. Prevention landed in the same session as `tests/test_bundled_mcps_bootable_static.py` — walks every bundled config, every MCP block (enabled AND disabled, since shipping the definition implies boot must work), and asserts `command`+`args` resolve via one of: `shutil.which` for bare names, `importlib.util.find_spec` for `-m <mod>`, absolute-path-exists for `python <script>`, executable-check for absolute commands. Relative paths fail with a pointed message naming `uv tool install` as the reason. Generalizable lesson: any bundled config entry that's a filesystem path has to be either (a) an absolute path resolvable via the package (`Path(operator.__file__).parent / "..."`), (b) a module-path invocation, or (c) a bare name on PATH. Everything else is latent rot. And the test suite has to exercise the *actual* bundled configs — synthetic YAML and synthetic MCP servers won't catch shipping-contract drift.
- **The inner `claude` CLI auto-appends its own `[workdir: \`...\`]\n[repo: \`...\`]` trailer when run with `--worktree` — separate from any wrapper-side footer.** (Session 162.) Session-126's wrapper added a transparency footer with workdir/repo/cost lines (no backticks). Session 162 removed that wrapper footer, but the same-shaped lines kept appearing in chat — until close inspection showed the new trailer had **backticks** around values (`` [workdir: `/tmp/...`] ``) and sometimes trailing two-space markdown line breaks. That's the inner `claude` CLI's own behavior — when invoked with `--worktree`, its response output includes a markdown-formatted breadcrumb noting where it ran. We don't pass any system prompt asking for it; the CLI does it natively. What made it tricky: the format was nearly identical to our own removed footer, so the obvious diagnosis ("my edit didn't land") was wrong; the fix was right but a *second* source was emitting the same noise. Confirmed by inspecting the actual `MCP tool result:` log content and noting the backtick formatting + markdown line-break trailing spaces — neither of which our wrapper used. Fix: regex-strip on the `result` string before returning to the LLM. The regex must be anchored to end-of-string with `\Z` so it can't strip a similar-looking pattern that legitimately appears mid-document (e.g., if a walkthrough quotes file paths in `[workdir: ...]` shape — unlikely but possible). `\n+\[workdir:\s*\`[^\`]+\`\][ \t]*\n\[repo:\s*\`[^\`]+\`\][ \t]*\Z`. Generalizable: when output looks "still wrong" after fixing one source, check for a second identical-looking emitter — especially when wrapping a CLI tool whose own behavior you don't fully control. The CLI's output format is part of its contract that can drift, so the strip regex should be tolerant of formatting variants (`[ \t]*` for trailing spaces, `\s*` after colons) but anchored at boundaries.
- **Skills that say "post each phase as its own message; don't pre-emit the whole thing" cause the model to literally stop mid-task and wait for a user prompt that's never coming.** (Session 162.) Five bundled skills (`codebase-walkthrough`, `live-bug-triage`, `migration-plan`, `prd-from-discussion`, `schedule-followup`) had Shape sections written when the architecture had no way to multi-message a reply — guidance was paragraph-formatting advice. Once we shipped streaming + paragraph-flush in the same session, those words became *literal* — the model started reading "one phase per message" as "emit phase 1, stop, wait for the user." First live walkthrough hit this: model produced hop 1 as ~96 tokens, then `end_turn`. User had to re-prompt to continue. What made it tricky: the streaming infra was working perfectly (paragraphs flushed cleanly), but the *content layer* had stale instructions that referenced an architectural constraint that had just been removed. Fix: rewrote four skills' Shape sections to "single turn, one phase per paragraph; harness streams each as a distinct Meet message; never stop and wait for the user to ask for the next part." Kept `schedule-followup` as-is (its two-message split is a side-effect confirmation gate for calendar create — correct UX, not the same anti-pattern). Generalizable: skill prompts written for one runtime can become wrong when the runtime gains capability — every skill that says "format X" or "split Y" is implicitly asserting a runtime constraint. After any harness-level rendering change, re-read every skill's Shape/Rules sections for instructions that conflict with the new behavior. And test the runtime + skills *together* — unit tests on the streaming layer alone would not have caught this; only the live walkthrough where a paragraph-formatted reply suddenly meant "stop here."
- **Rich's `Prompt.ask` captures raw stdin bytes including ESC; `.strip()` doesn't remove them; YAML serializes them as `"\e"`.** (Session 162.) User's `~/.operator/agents/claude/config.yaml` showed `personality: "\e"` despite never having typed those characters. Reproduction: in the wizard's step 4 system-prompt input, press Escape (intending to skip / cancel), press Enter. Rich captured the single ESC byte (`\x1b`, also written `\e`) as the input string. The wizard's `.strip()` only removes ASCII whitespace, not control characters, so the byte survived. `if new_text:` evaluated True (single char is truthy). The byte got written to YAML, where PyYAML serialized it as the escape sequence `"\e"`. Then at runtime, that single ESC char became part of the bot's `SYSTEM_PROMPT`, contributing nothing useful but also displacing the empty-personality-default behavior the model would otherwise hit. Symptom downstream: the bot improvised its own name on intro ("Goose", "Dingus") because the system prompt had no anchoring text. What made it tricky: the corruption was invisible in normal use (the file looked fine in editors that render escape sequences) and only surfaced via the downstream symptom of broken intros. The intro itself was a separate bug (prompt didn't reference `AGENT_NAME`), so fixing one without fixing the other was insufficient. Fix: stripped control chars in `_prompt_with_hint` via `_CONTROL_CHARS_RE = re.compile(r"[\x00-\x1f\x7f]")` before returning, so the same accidental ESC press now produces an empty string and the `if new_text:` branch correctly preserves the preset's defaults. Cleared the existing bad value to `personality: ''`. Generalizable: any input function that captures raw stdin needs explicit control-char filtering — `.strip()` is not enough. This applies to API key inputs, custom skill paths, and any other wizard field. Apply the filter centrally in the lowest-level input wrapper, not at each call site, so future fields are protected by default.
- **Wizard-time modules can't `from operator import config` — config.py asserts `OPERATOR_BOT` at import.** (Session 159.) First run of the new Phase 14.10 wizard step died at `operator setup` with `ERROR: OPERATOR_BOT env var is not set.` — not a runtime check, the assertion fired on `from _1_800_operator.pipeline.setup import run` because `setup.py` now imports `google_signin.py`, which had `from operator import config` at module top, which trips line 13 of `config.py` before the wizard even starts. The wizard's whole purpose is to choose a bot, so by definition `OPERATOR_BOT` is unset when it runs. Pattern was already documented in `setup.py`'s comment around `_ENV_FILE`: "Inlined rather than imported from _1_800_operator.config because config.py triggers a OPERATOR_BOT gate at import time and the wizard runs before a bot is chosen." Fix: same compromise — inline the three path constants (`_BROWSER_PROFILE_DIR`, `_AUTH_STATE_FILE`, `_GOOGLE_ACCOUNT_FILE`) in `google_signin.py`, plus a private `_auth_state_has_sid()` helper that mirrors `connectors.session.validate_auth_state` without the import chain (validate_auth_state itself is fine to call at runtime since session.py only imports config lazily under `save_debug`, but importing it still drags `from operator import config` at session.py's module top). Three-way regression guard added (`test_google_signin_paths_canonical_across_modules`) to fail loud if anyone updates `config.GOOGLE_ACCOUNT_FILE` without lockstep-updating `google_signin._GOOGLE_ACCOUNT_FILE`. Lesson: any module imported during wizard execution (anything reachable from `_run_setup`'s import graph) is bound by the same constraint — must not transitively import config. When adding pre-bot-selection wizard code, run `OPERATOR_BOT= python -c "..."` to verify the import chain is clean before live-testing.
- **Playwright bundled "Chrome for Testing" can't open profiles created by real `Google Chrome.app` — process dies on launch with no useful error.** (Session 159.) New `_launch_signin_flow` opened `~/.operator/browser_profile/` via `p.chromium.launch_persistent_context(user_data_dir=...)` without setting `executable_path`. Default Playwright path is the bundled `Google Chrome for Testing` binary (`~/Library/Caches/ms-playwright/chromium-1208/chrome-mac-arm64/...`). The user's `browser_profile/` was created by real `Google Chrome.app` per `macos_adapter.py:512` (`executable_path="/Applications/Google Chrome.app/Contents/MacOS/Google Chrome"`). Chrome refuses to open profiles across binary versions/distributions — launched process exited immediately with `kill EPERM` and the Playwright API surfaced `BrowserType.launch_persistent_context: Target page, context or browser has been closed`. The actual cause was visible only in the verbose browser-logs dump as a cryptic `<gracefully close start>` followed by `<process did exit: exitCode=null, signal=SIGTRAP>` — the SIGTRAP comes from Chrome's own profile-version mismatch protection. Fix: pass `executable_path="/Applications/Google Chrome.app/Contents/MacOS/Google Chrome"` to match the adapter exactly, so the wizard seeds a profile the runtime adapter can read and vice versa. Generalizable: when any Playwright path touches the persistent profile, the binary identity must be the same one the runtime uses — there's no portability across Chrome distributions. (Cross-platform implication: a future Linux adapter that uses `chromium` as the persistent-profile binary will need its own `executable_path` resolution; bundled Chrome-for-Testing might be acceptable on Linux but the choice has to be locked once and matched everywhere.)
- **Rich `Prompt.ask` with `[letter]` markup eats brackets even in label text.** (Session 159.) Same trap as session 119's `[x]` bug, recurred in a different shape. Initial detect-branch prompt was `Prompt.ask("[enter] continue with this account · [r] sign in with a different account", choices=["", "r"], default="")`. Rendered as garbled `" continue with this account ·  sign in with a different account"` — Rich parsed `[enter]` and `[r]` as nonexistent style tags and stripped them. User couldn't tell what to type. Fix: reframed as conventional yes/no on the *non-default* path: `Prompt.ask("Sign in with a different account?", choices=["y", "n"], default="n")`. Cleaner UX too — matches git/brew/apt convention, default = continue with current, capitalized N in the rendered `[y/n] (n)` cues the safe choice. Generalizable: any time you find yourself writing `[word]` or `[letter]` inside Rich text, either escape with `\[`, use unicode brackets like `「」` or parentheses, or — better — refactor to a yes/no with `choices=...` so Rich renders the affordance for you. Don't fight Rich's markup parser, work with its prompt API.
- **Rich `Panel(width=N)` is NOT an absolute width — it caps, not locks, unless `expand=True`.** (Session 153.) User reported the build card "expanding and contracting with each selection" during the setup wizard. First reading of the code looked correct: `Panel(inner, width=40, padding=(0,0))`. But Rich's default `expand=True` makes the panel fill available width in its container *up to* `width=`; with `expand=False`, the panel *shrinks to fit content* up to `width=`. Neither is "always exactly N cells." Rich models width as a ceiling, not a fixed value. So when the wizard's `Table.grid` left column was narrow (short "custom" tagline) the panel hit the ceiling from above and stretched to 40; when it was wide (long preset tagline) the panel could have stretched further but the cap held it at 40; and short-content renders with `expand=False` shrank the panel to content-length. User read this as "expanding and contracting" because subjectively the card *did* change size between renders. What made it tricky: naïve fix was `expand=False` — which actually made it worse because now the "custom" card rendered narrower than preset cards (shrink-to-content). The right fix is `expand=True` + `width=N` together: expand fills up to the ceiling, ceiling is explicit, result is truly fixed. Also discovered downstream: `Panel(width=40)` inside a `Table.grid` cell can still get clipped on narrow terminals if `left_col_min + padding + 40 > console.size.width` — Rich has to cut something and chooses the panel's right border/content. Fix there was terminal-aware sizing (`build_card.width_for(console)` clamps between `MIN_WIDTH=28` and `DEFAULT_WIDTH=40` based on `console.size.width - 34`). Generalizable: whenever a Rich layout needs a truly fixed dimension, always pair `width=N` with `expand=True` AND ensure the parent layout has enough room — the "just set width" intuition from CSS/Tk doesn't transfer. Also: every Rich component that takes `width` should be re-checked for `expand` semantics when the layout evolves, because `expand`'s default varies by component.
- **Bundled-vs-user config drift silently ignores your edits — `_ensure_user_agents` only seeds missing bots, never updates.** (Session 156.) User flipped `captions_enabled: true` in the *bundled* `src/_1_800_operator/agents/claude/config.yaml`, restarted the bot, and found the caption pipeline still dark in `/tmp/operator.log` (no `caption bridge exposed`, no `caption: [...]`, no transcript wiring). The runtime copy at `~/.operator/agents/claude/config.yaml` still had `captions_enabled: false` — the runtime reads that file, not the bundled one. Root cause: `_ensure_user_agents` at `src/_1_800_operator/__main__.py:95` uses `if not dest.exists(): shutil.copytree(bundled, dest)` — a per-bot, create-only seed. Deliberately additive so wizard edits and hand-tweaks are never stomped, but the cost is that *any* bundled-config edit post-first-install is invisible to existing installs. What made it tricky: the diagnosis was upside-down. Initial instinct was "check config.py's CAPTIONS_ENABLED wiring" or "maybe the caption observer failed to inject." The actual fix was "read the file the runtime actually loads, not the one in your editor." The log silence was the key signal — had the caption pipeline wired and failed at the JS-bridge or MutationObserver layer, there would have been log lines from `MacOSAdapter: caption bridge exposed` or `captions: Shift+C failed`. Nothing from the caption subsystem at all meant nothing was wired, which meant `config.CAPTIONS_ENABLED` was `False` at import time, which meant the on-disk file had `false` regardless of what was in the editor. Compounding: the bundled config's header comment said "Captions off by default — Claude's value is chat-directed delegation, not ambient transcript parsing" while the value itself was `true` (dev-time flip, comment left stale). So even if you noticed the file the dev hat was on, the comment would tell you the intended default was `false`, reinforcing the wrong mental model. Fix: (a) edit the runtime copy for the session, (b) rewrite the bundled comment to match the `true` value, (c) add `operator edit <bot|.env>` and `operator where <bot|.env>` subcommands (commit `01b0868`) so users of any install shape can reach the runtime config in one command and never repeat this "I edited the wrong file" loop. Generalizable lesson: whenever a feature silently doesn't happen at runtime, the fastest debug path is "grep `/tmp/operator.log` for any log line from the subsystem" — presence vs absence of subsystem log lines discriminates "wiring missing" from "wiring failed" faster than reading the code does, and drift bugs between bundled and user config always present as the former. Open architectural gap: `operator setup --sync-bundled` would surface bundled-to-user drift with per-key accept/skip prompts; not yet built. Dev-mode symlink (`~/.operator/agents/<bot>` → `src/_1_800_operator/agents/<bot>`) would let bundled edits hit runtime immediately during development; also not yet built.
- **Agent recall of external-tool API surface drifts; only the actually-installed binary is authoritative.** (Session 165.) Phase 14.12.1 spike: two separate research agents both reported that Claude Code's CLI exposes a `--permission-prompt-tool` flag that delegates permission requests to a named MCP tool. First agent gave a complete request/response JSON contract for it; second agent (asked for verification) walked it back partially but still placed the flag in some configs. Direct `claude --help` on the actually-installed CLI showed the flag does NOT exist. The real CLI mechanism is PreToolUse hooks via `--settings <file-or-json>`. Inverse drift on the same docs source: `code.claude.com` settings docs claim "the `--settings` flag does not exist" — but `claude --help` shows it does (`--settings <file-or-json>  Path to a settings JSON file or a JSON string to load additional settings from`). What made it tricky: agents are confidently wrong in opposite directions on consecutive lookups, with "evidence" that looks reasonable on surface read. The fix wasn't more research — it was running `claude --help | grep -i permission` and `claude --help | grep -i setting` directly against the binary. Generalizable rule: when integrating with another tool's CLI/SDK, the binary's own `--help` output and the SDK's installed source code are authoritative; agent-recalled docs and even the official docs site can be stale or wrong. Build a probe before committing architecture decisions to research.
- **A probe that hangs is ambiguous between "blocking on a missing decision" vs. "doing slow work" — disambiguate with a different permission mode.** (Session 165.) Probe 2 (sub-agent permission visibility) asked inner-claude to dispatch a Task sub-agent that creates two files. Hook fired once for the parent's `Agent` dispatch; we approved. Then nothing for 180s. Files were not written; tmp dir empty. Two interpretations: (a) sub-agent's tool calls bypass our hook AND are blocked on some other interactive permission gate we can't see; (b) sub-agent is just slowly thinking and would eventually fire hooks if we waited long enough. Both look identical from the outside. Disambiguation: probe 2b — same scenario but with `--permission-mode bypassPermissions`. If sub-agent's writes go through silently without firing our hook, they're definitively bypassing it; if they DO fire our hook, the parent mode just changes whether interactive prompts appear. Probe 2b ran in 137s (vs 180s timeout in probe 2), produced both files, and our hook fired exactly once — for the top-level `Agent` dispatch only. Sub-agent Writes were invisible to us. That confirmed interpretation (a). Generalizable: when probing a CLI tool's permission/auth/IO behavior and a probe hangs ambiguously, run the same scenario under the most-permissive mode you can — if it completes quickly, the difference between fast and hung is *what permission decisions are pending*, which tells you exactly what gates are firing where. The bypass mode is a diagnostic tool, not just a security setting.
- **`ANTHROPIC_API_KEY` set in `~/.operator/.env` silently leaks into every spawned MCP subprocess and re-auths the inner `claude -p` away from the user's Max subscription onto API billing.** (Session 164, late.) User re-upped $5 in API credits, was out by morning. Console showed $6.50 spend; operator's outer LLM accounted for only $2.87 (Sonnet 4.5). The other $3.64 (Sonnet 4.6 + Haiku 4.5) was unaccounted by `/tmp/operator.log` reconstruction — making it look like another tool was billing the same key, or the key was compromised. Real cause traced via `~/.operator/debug/claude-stream.jsonl` showing `apiKeySource: ANTHROPIC_API_KEY` + `model: claude-sonnet-4-6` in the inner-claude system init event: every `delegate_to_claude_code` call's inner CLI was billing API rates instead of consuming the user's Max subscription. Leak path: operator's process loads `ANTHROPIC_API_KEY` via `load_dotenv(~/.operator/.env)` into `os.environ`; `pipeline/mcp_client.py:593` spawns each MCP server with `env={**os.environ, **self._srv_config["env"]}`; the claude-code MCP server then spawns the inner `claude -p --worktree` subprocess which inherits the env again; inner claude CLI's auth-precedence rule prefers `ANTHROPIC_API_KEY` over the claude.ai login when both are present. What made it tricky: `claude auth status` reported `authMethod: claude.ai, subscriptionType: max` on the user's interactive shell (because `ANTHROPIC_API_KEY` is *not* set in the user's shell env — only in operator's process env after `load_dotenv`), so every surface-level check said "you're on Max." Inner claude only flipped to API billing when run as a subprocess of operator, where the env was different. Console-side, the spend looked like it could be a leaked or shared key — but the API-key filter showed only one key in use, so the leak had to be coming from within operator's own call chain. The Claude Code dashboard's `[API KEY]` label on the workspace member was the first concrete tell that *something* on the user's machine was using API auth despite the Max subscription being in place. Confirmed by reading the stream-json side-log written by the same session's commit `d0ff5a7` (which we'd added for live worktree progress, not for cost diagnosis — paid for itself the same session). Generalizable: any time API spend doesn't match the local log, look at *every* subprocess in the call chain — env var inheritance is the silent killer. Anthropic's auth-precedence rule (env var > stored credentials) means a subprocess with the env can override the parent's UI auth without any signal. Mitigation paths considered: (a) surgical env-strip in `agents/engineer/claude_code.py:_run_claude` (rejected — was the patch fix, not the architectural one); (b) full pivot to track-A architecture where inner claude IS the outer LLM (chosen — Phase 14.12). For future MCP servers spawned from operator that wrap subprocesses with auth state, the same env-leak risk applies: explicitly construct the env dict, don't inherit os.environ wholesale. Also the *broader* lesson: API rates aren't a hike — Max subscription is heavily subsidized for the Claude Code persona (flat fee, metered by messages-per-window, Anthropic absorbs marginal token cost). The same task that's free under Max costs $1–3 on API. Most users assume API is the canonical price and subscription wraps it. It's the opposite. Worth surfacing in the README + wizard copy.
- **Synthetic tool_results that *sound* like real output get hallucinated as "success — output is X".** (Session 164.) When the user replies non-yes during a pending tool confirmation, `chat_runner._handle_confirmation` had to send a synthetic `tool_result` back to Anthropic to satisfy the strict spec rule that every `tool_use` block requires a paired `tool_result`. Original wording was "User wants to adjust this call and said: '<text>' — re-propose the corrected tool call." Sounded like guidance. Anthropic read it as a regular tool result — the model concluded the tool ran successfully and produced this text, then wrote *"delegation is running in that repo now. Let me wait for the results!"* even though no MCP execution had happened (no `MCP executing tool=` line in the log for that turn). The user then asked "Did you get results?" and the LLM re-issued the tool_call, prompting a second confirmation that did fire correctly. What made it tricky: the flow looked like a legitimate confirmation loop from the user's POV (they were prompted twice, approved once, work eventually happened) but the first ask had been a phantom — no tool ran, the LLM made up the "running" status. Fix: prefix the synthetic result with `[TOOL NOT EXECUTED — user did not approve the call.]` + explicit "no output exists" + 2-option directive ("Either re-propose a corrected tool call, or ask the user a clarifying question"). The bracket tag is a strong machine-readable signal Anthropic's training apparently respects; reframed text now produces correct re-propose behavior. Generalizable: any synthetic message you inject as a `tool_result` will be read by the model as *if a tool produced it*. Frame it accordingly — leading bracket tags, explicit "NOT executed", explicit "no output", and a list of valid next moves. Don't write tool_result content as if it's instruction; the model treats it as data.
- **Stuck-LLM watchdog message with trailing `\n\n` self-reply cascaded because Meet's DOM strips trailing whitespace.** (Session 164.) New watchdog timer posted `"Anthropic is taking longer than usual to respond — hang tight.\n\n"` via the streaming `on_paragraph` callback. Meet's chat panel DOM stripped trailing whitespace on render, so when the same message came back through the MutationObserver-fed `read_chat`, the text was `"...hang tight."` (no `\n\n`). ChatRunner's own-message dedup was `text in self._own_messages` — exact equality. The two strings didn't match, the bot processed its own watchdog as a fresh user message, the LLM replied with the same text, that got read back as another new user message, looped 3 times before the user noticed. What made it tricky: every visible signal looked normal — chat showed the watchdog message exactly once, the model's reply looked like a reasonable acknowledgement; only `/tmp/operator.log` showed the cascade as `new message sender='' id='...' text='Anthropic is taking longer...'` followed by a healthy `TIMING llm_call=2.6s` for the LLM "replying" to itself. Sender came back empty because the MutationObserver's sender-walk (look up 4 parents for a sibling div with timestamp) doesn't always find a name node for own-bot messages, so it returned ''. Two-pronged fix: (a) immediate — drop the `\n\n` from the watchdog text since it's a one-shot notice not a streamed paragraph chain; (b) structural — have `connector.send_chat` return the new `data-message-id` (macos_adapter snapshots pre-send IDs, polls 50ms × 20 for the new one) so chat_runner can dedup by ID rather than text. Plus stripped-text fallback for adapters that can't return an ID (linux). Generalizable: never rely on exact text match for own-message detection across a render boundary — the DOM is allowed to normalize anything (trailing whitespace, smart quotes, NFC form, &nbsp; collapse). ID-based dedup is the only durable primitive; text-match is a fallback that should always normalize both sides. And: trailing `\n\n` on a string going through an `on_paragraph` callback is a code smell — the contract is "complete paragraph without separators," and the watchdog accidentally violated it.
- **Cute placeholder heartbeats actively hurt UX once you have a real progress signal.** (Session 164.) Session 161 added a 123-verb pool (Embossing… Frolicking… Hornswoggling…) as heartbeats during long tool calls. Made the bot feel alive when the only alternative was complete silence for 5 minutes during a claude-code delegate. But once stream-json gave us real inner-tool_use events ("Reading config.py… Editing chat_runner.py… Running: pytest tests/test_x.py"), the verbs became dead weight — they *interfered* with the real signal. Worse, for non-claude-code tools (linear/github queries that complete in <1s), the verb tick had been firing exactly never anyway, so removing it for those was zero behavior change. Generalizable: placeholder UX (loading spinners, fake progress, generic heartbeats) is a stopgap that has a strict shelf life. The moment real progress signal exists, kill the placeholder — it doesn't compose with truth, it competes with it. And don't be sentimental about the deleted code: 123 hand-curated verbs made for a charming PR but are zero loss when the real signal arrives.

- **Mirror the source tool's scope-walking precedence, not just its top-level/user view.** (Session 172.) Operator's `extract_imported_mcps` walked `~/.claude.json#mcpServers` (user scope) and silently dropped per-project entries at `~/.claude.json#projects.<cwd>.mcpServers`. `claude mcp add` *defaults* to project scope, so most user-added MCPs lived there; meanwhile `claude mcp list` (which operator also calls as a CLI fallback) merges both scopes for the current cwd before printing. The user noticed because Sentry was missing from the imported set in the wild, then a contrived `claude mcp add demo --` test confirmed: operator never saw it. Same blind spot existed for skills (`~/.claude/skills/` only; `<cwd>/.claude/skills/` ignored) and CLAUDE.md (`~/.claude/CLAUDE.md` only; project CLAUDE.md ignored). What made it tricky: each integration point looked correct in isolation, and the existing tests all used user-scope fixtures so coverage was complete-but-from-the-wrong-angle. The bug only surfaces when the user's actual config splits across scopes — which is the *normal* case for any modern multi-project CLI tool. Generalizable: when integrating with another tool's config layer, the first acceptance test should be "does my code see what `<other tool> list` sees?", not "does my code parse the canonical config file?". The tool's own merging logic — user scope ∪ cwd-scoped project — is the contract, and unit tests against synthetic single-scope dicts won't catch it.

- **Order safeguards so a failure in any later step never leaves the file in a worse state than before.** (Session 172.) Session 171 added five YAML lifecycle safeguards (atomic write, schema validation, ruamel round-trip, per-entry merge, MCP failure surface) but ordered them as "merge → atomic write → validate." When a user's pre-existing config had a corrupt `permissions.always_ask` (`{'Taskmcp_servers': None}` from a missing newline in an earlier hand-edit), the per-entry merge happily succeeded against the still-buggy parse, the atomic writer wrote a new correct `mcp_servers:` block at top level, and *only then* did the validator reject the still-malformed permissions entry and exit. Net result: the user's file went from "one malformed line" to "one malformed line plus a duplicate orphan import block at the bottom" — and the boot still failed. Each individual safeguard worked exactly as designed in isolation, but the composition turned a single-corruption case into a doubly-broken file. The "atomic" in atomic-write only protects against torn writes, not against writing a known-bad shape. What made it tricky: every test the safeguards landed with passed (each tested its own step in isolation against synthetic inputs), and the failure required a specific sequence — pre-existing corruption + merge-changes-needed + validation-would-have-caught-it. Generalizable: any time you stack safeguards on a file lifecycle, draw the dependency arrow on paper. Validation should always run before any write that could persist the validated-bad state. Better order: parse → validate raw → merge → validate post-merge → atomic write. The atomic-write step is the *last* line of defense, not a substitute for "should I be writing this at all?"

- **CLI text output: when scraping, sample multiple entry types — same column structure can have variant decorations the parser must tolerate.** (Session 172.) `claude mcp list` formats most entries as `name: URL - status` (e.g. `claude.ai Linear: https://mcp.linear.app/sse - ✓ Connected`). Operator's regex `^(?P<name>.+?):\s+(?P<url>https?://\S+)\s+-\s+(?P<status>.+?)\s*$` matched this perfectly. But for HTTP-not-SSE remote MCPs, the CLI inserts a `(HTTP)` annotation between the URL and the dash: `sentry: https://mcp.sentry.dev/mcp (HTTP) - ✓ Connected`. The regex's `\s+-\s+` literal silently failed to match, dropping the entire line. SSE-only test fixtures meant the unit test passed; the bug only surfaced in the wild when sentry (the most common HTTP-not-SSE entry users actually configure) was silently missing from the import. Fix: `(?:\s+\([A-Z]+\))?` between URL and dash. What made it tricky: the regex looks like it should handle "everything after the URL" but the literal ` - ` is brittle to any annotation injected by the upstream tool. Generalizable: when freezing a regex against CLI output, sample at least two structurally-distinct entry types (HTTP vs SSE here, but also: connected vs disconnected, with vs without optional fields, single-name vs multi-word display names). The columns that look uniform from one example are often where the upstream tool quietly varies decoration. Test fixtures should mirror the real shape diversity, not just the canonical one.

- **Tool-confirmation prompts self-poisoned the LLM context when persisted as `kind="chat"`.** (Session 127.) `ChatRunner._request_confirmation` sends "I'd like to run X. OK?" into chat and `_send` appended it to the meeting record. On the next turn, `MeetingRecord.tail()` replayed it to the model as a prior assistant message — the model learned to mimic the harness's own wording as *plain text* before emitting a real `tool_call`. Symptoms: (a) the user saw two confirmation prompts per call (a hallucinated one from the model, then the real one from the harness) and had to type "ok" twice; (b) occasionally after the user's "ok", the model skipped the real tool_call and just hallucinated a result — caught during Phase 15.5.7 step 6 smoke when Engineer reported "README.md" as the contents of a repo that actually held `f.txt`. What made it tricky: no single line in the code was wrong — each piece (persist chat to record, replay tail to model, prompt for confirmation in chat) was correct in isolation; the bug was an emergent feedback loop from all three composing. Looked like a prompt-engineering problem at first (add a "don't parrot confirmations" nudge to system prompt) until reframed as "why are harness prompts in the LLM's prompt at all?". Fix: `_send` gained a `kind` kwarg; `_request_confirmation` passes `kind="confirmation"`; user replies while `_pending_tool_call` is set are also tagged `kind="confirmation"` in `_loop`. `pipeline/llm.py` already filtered `tail()` to `kind in {chat, caption}`, so one-word keyword change made the whole harness-plumbing layer invisible to the model while keeping the full audit trail in the JSONL. Generalizable: any text the harness *shows in chat for UX reasons* is not the same as a conversation turn — keep them in separate lanes from day one, or you'll get mimicry bugs that masquerade as model hallucinations.


- **Meet renders chat messages only after a participant opens the chat panel — posting before that means the message is invisible to humans even though it landed in the DOM.** (Session 174.) Added a `Hold for {AGENT_NAME}...` line that fires immediately after `connector.join()` returns success. User reported it never appeared in the chat panel. The send_chat call was succeeding — the message ID came back, the DOM had the entry — but Meet's chat panel only paints messages received *after* the user opens it; messages that land while chat is closed are silently dropped from the rendered list. The bot joins instantly; the human takes 5–15s to clear pre-join + open chat. The intro pipeline already worked around this by gating on `saw_others` (a human is in the room), but the new hold line was firing unconditionally on join. Fix: move the hold-line send into the main loop, gated on the same `saw_others` check the intro uses. What made it tricky: the bug looks like a send_chat failure (no error, no return value issue, no log line out of place) but is actually a *Meet UI behavior*. The DOM has the message; the user just can't see it. Easy to chase down the wrong stack (chat-queue races, send_chat failures, MutationObserver timing) before realizing it's a render-time platform behavior. Generalizable: any chat post that needs to land in front of a human must wait until at least one human has been seen in the meeting — Meet's render-from-open-time semantics make pre-arrival posts invisible.

- **`uv tool run --from playwright playwright install` and `uv tool run --from "git+<operator>" playwright install` download chromium to *different* version-keyed cache dirs — they are NOT interchangeable.** (Session 190.) install.sh was emitting a cosmetic uv warning during step 5: *"An executable named `playwright` is not provided by package `1-800-operator` but is available via the dependency `playwright`. Consider using `uv tool run --from playwright playwright` instead."* Took the suggestion at face value, switched the flag — install passed cleanly. Days later, the `dial` command on a fresh Mac hit `Executable doesn't exist at .../chromium_headless_shell-1208/chrome-headless-shell-mac-arm64/chrome-headless-shell`. Root cause: `--from playwright` makes uv resolve **latest** PyPI playwright in an ephemeral env, downloads chromium build keyed to that version into `~/Library/Caches/ms-playwright/`. But operator's pinned dep is `playwright==1.58.0`, and 1.58 looks for chromium at a *different* version-keyed path. Cache shared, paths divergent, no overlap → "executable doesn't exist." The original `--from "git+${REPO_URL}"` form forced uv to resolve operator's full dep tree (pinned 1.58.0) and download the matching chromium. The "warning" we silenced was telling us "1-800-operator doesn't expose `playwright` as a script entry point" — true, but irrelevant; uv still finds it via the dep graph. What made this tricky: the install completed without errors. The bug only surfaced at first dial, days later, far from the install code. The cosmetic warning ("consider using…") was *worded as a recommendation*, but the recommendation broke version alignment that was load-bearing. Generalizable: when wrapping a tool that has version-keyed on-disk artifacts (browser binaries, model weights, generated bindings), **the version that does the install MUST be the version that runs at use-time**. If those two are different uv invocations, route both through the same dep-resolution source. Cosmetic warnings are not always cosmetic.

- **`meet.new` doesn't always redirect to a real meeting URL — on a fresh persistent profile it can sit on `/new` showing a "Continue without microphone and camera" interstitial that has to be clicked first.** (Session 190.) After `page.goto("https://meet.new")`, `wait_for_url(_is_real_meet_room)` was timing out at 30s on a fresh Mac, while working instantly on the dev Mac. The user's debug dumps showed the page WAS reachable (canonical href = `meet.google.com/fbk-ccxn-drd` in one run), but in another the URL stayed at `meet.google.com/new?authuser=0&hs=178&pli=1` with the page showing a *"Do you want people to see and hear you in the meeting?"* heading and a *"Continue without microphone and camera"* button. The adapter already had click-the-button logic — but it ran AFTER `wait_for_url(_is_real_meet_room)` succeeded. Chicken-and-egg: the URL won't redirect until the button is clicked, and we won't try to click the button until the URL redirects. Replaced the single `wait_for_url` with a 30s poll watching for either (a) URL becomes a real meeting room, or (b) "Continue without microphone and camera" button appears — clicks it when it does. Once dismissed, Meet completes the redirect normally. What made this tricky: dev Macs have warm Meet state (cookies, prior device-permission grants on the visible Chrome profile, possibly cached service-worker state) that makes Meet skip the interstitial entirely. The bug was invisible until QA against a never-used machine. Also: the `--use-fake-device-for-media-stream` Chromium flag (added the same session to bypass macOS-level mic prompts) doesn't fool Meet — it still detects something off about the synthesized device feed and shows the interstitial as a soft block. Generalizable: any browser-automation flow that depends on a redirect chain on a fresh profile needs to poll for both the success condition AND any plausible interstitials in parallel — never assume the redirect is the only thing that can hold the URL stable. Test against a never-used persistent profile before claiming the flow works.

- **`uv tool install` is fully isolated from any other Python environment on the user's machine — version conflicts are a non-concern unless the user manually deletes the shared chromium cache.** (Session 190.) User asked: "what if a user already has Playwright but a different version than what we pinned?" The answer is *nothing* — operator's tool venv at `~/.local/share/uv/tools/1-800-operator/` is an isolated install with its own playwright==1.58.0; system pip, pipx, conda, other project venvs are all invisible to it. The only shared resource is `~/Library/Caches/ms-playwright/` (chromium binary cache), which is *additive*: each chromium build gets its own version-keyed subdir (`chromium-1208/`, `chromium-1212/`, etc.), so multiple Playwright versions coexist by storing each build separately. Cost is disk, not correctness — ~170 MB per build. The one real edge case: a user running `playwright uninstall --all` from another venv would nuke the shared cache including operator's chromium, after which `operator dial` fails with "Executable doesn't exist" until the user re-runs `playwright install chromium` (in operator's venv specifically: `~/.local/share/uv/tools/1-800-operator/bin/playwright install chromium`). Latent footgun also: future operator version bumps to playwright pin would need install.sh's step 5 to re-fire, which it does on `curl|bash` but NOT on `uv tool upgrade 1-800-operator` — so power users who upgrade via uv directly might skip the chromium re-download and hit the same "doesn't exist" error at next dial. Worth a Phase 16+ fix (run `playwright install chromium` from a post-install hook on the operator package itself, or have `operator dial` lazily-install chromium if missing). Generalizable: when shipping a CLI via a tool installer (uv, pipx, brew), users have a mental model of "install command = once-and-done." If your install requires multiple steps (binary download + companion artifact download), and the package manager only handles step 1, document the gap or wire it into a runtime preflight.

- **macOS ScreenCaptureKit's `CGPreflightScreenCaptureAccess()` returns true even when frames will not flow — the responsible-process attribution chain matters more than the calling-process grant.** (Session 193, Phase 14.20.1 spike.) Spike binary at `debug/14_20_audio_spike/spike_capture.swift` was producing zero PCM bytes in 8 seconds of audible `say` output despite preflight returning true and `SCStream.startCapture()` succeeding without error. First hypothesis was that Cursor (the IDE running the test shell) lacked Screen Recording — but the user confirmed Cursor was granted in System Settings. Diagnosis: macOS's TCC system attaches grants to a *responsible process* identified by code signature (cdhash), and that responsible process is computed by walking up the spawn ancestry. The spike binary's chain was `spike_capture → zsh → claude → zsh → Cursor Helper: terminal pty-host → Cursor.app`. The "Cursor Helper: terminal pty-host" is a SEPARATE bundle from `Cursor.app` proper, with its own bundle ID. Granting "Cursor" Screen Recording grants the main app bundle; the helper is unrelated and not in the granted list. `CGPreflightScreenCaptureAccess()` returns true because *some* ancestor in the chain has the grant (or the TCC daemon has a stale "yes" cached for the binary's cdhash from a prior interaction); but the kernel's frame-delivery enforcement is stricter and checks the immediate-responsible-process bundle, finds it ungranted, and silently drops every frame. **Compounding factor:** the spike binary was ad-hoc signed (`codesign -dv` showed `Signature=adhoc, TeamIdentifier=not set`), so every `swiftc` recompile produces a NEW cdhash, which TCC treats as a fresh subject — even an explicit grant on the binary itself is invalidated on the next compile. What made this tricky: three layers conspired (helper-bundle attribution, ad-hoc cdhash churn, preflight-vs-frame-delivery asymmetry), and any one of them in isolation would be hard to spot; together they produce "preflight returns true + startCapture succeeds + no callbacks" which looks like an API failure rather than a permission failure. Tried running from `Terminal.app` instead of via Cursor's pty — also produced 0 bytes (Terminal.app didn't have the grant either, and we couldn't easily verify the responsible-process chain there). Generalizable for 14.20.2: (a) Developer-ID code-sign the production helper binary so it gets a stable identity that survives recompiles — this is a day-one shipping requirement, not a polish item; (b) ship a 10-second no-callback watchdog as cheap insurance for a smaller class of edge cases (Sequoia weekly re-prompt, TCC daemon cache staleness across macOS updates, helper-bundle drift). The watchdog framing as "macOS will lie to your face" was overstated — what actually breaks is attribution-chain edge cases, not normal granted operation; but the watchdog is still cheap and the user-visible failure mode (silent bot) is bad enough to justify it.

- **`ad-hoc` Swift binaries cannot retain TCC grants across recompiles, period.** (Session 193, Phase 14.20.1 spike.) Specific corollary of the silent-failure entry above, worth its own line because it directly shapes 14.20.2's build pipeline. `swiftc spike_capture.swift -o spike_capture` produces a Mach-O thin binary with `Signature=adhoc, linker-signed, hashes=22+0`. Each rebuild changes the cdhash. macOS TCC keys grants on cdhash + bundle id (where applicable). For an ad-hoc binary with no bundle, the cdhash IS the identity. Every `swiftc` invocation creates a "different binary" from TCC's perspective. This means: dev-loop iteration on the helper requires re-granting permissions every recompile; users who upgrade operator will see TCC re-prompt on every release; an attacker who replaces the binary cannot reuse the prior grant (security feature). Fix: sign with a stable Developer ID Application certificate before shipping. The Developer ID identity stays constant across all binaries signed by the same developer, so TCC sees "different cdhash but same Developer ID = same app, preserve grant." Cost: requires an Apple Developer account ($99/year + DUNS number prep), `codesign --sign "Developer ID Application: ..."` step in the build pipeline, plus optionally `notarize` + `staple` for first-run UX (no Gatekeeper warning). All of these need to be set up *before* 14.20.2 ships its first release — once users have ad-hoc-signed helpers with grants, switching to Developer ID will renag everyone exactly once before stabilizing. Generalizable: any macOS binary that requests TCC permissions and ships through a non-App-Store distribution channel must have stable code-signing as a hard prerequisite, not a polish item. This applies to CLI tools, helper daemons, anything spawned by a shell that's expected to access mic/screen/camera/contacts/photos.
